Exemptions from Jizya: Categories and Proofs
States categories exempt from jizya with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the categories exempt from jizya in the Hanafi school and identify the textual proofs (dalil) for each exemption.

Core Idea
Jizya is only taken from free adult able-bodied males; women, children, disabled, poor, and isolated monks are exempt.

The jizya is a poll tax on non-Muslim subjects, but it is not universal. The Hanafi school, based on explicit hadith and athar, exempts specific categories who cannot earn or are not part of the workforce. This lesson unpacks each exemption and its scriptural basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exempt categories
Read the matn and list the six categories: women, boys (children before puberty), the crippled (zamin), the blind, the poor who cannot work (faqir ghayr mu’tamil), and monks who do not mix with people.
2
Locate the proof for each category
For women and children: the hadith of Muadh (taking jizya from every ‘halim’ i.e., mature adult) and the athar of Umar (explicitly excluding women and boys). For the elderly: athar of Umar refusing jizya from a very old dhimmi. For the disabled (crippled/blind): qiyas to the elderly, as they are similarly unable to work. For monks: the treaty of Khalid ibn al-Walid with the people of Hira (not directly quoted but referenced in commentary).
The commentary uses qiyas for the disabled.
3
Understand the underlying reason (illah)
The common factor is inability to earn or being withdrawn from worldly life. Jizya is imposed on those capable of paying and participating in society.
4
Note the Hanafi consensus
These exemptions are agreed upon within the Hanafi school (zahir al-riwayah) and are mufta bihi.
No intra-Hanafi dispute.

بوڑھے پر جزیہ نہیں ہے اس کی دلیل یہ اثر ہے۔ قال ابصر عمر شيخا كبيرا من اهل الذمة يسأل فقال له مالك؟ قال ليس لى مال وان الجزية توخذ منى فقال له عمر ما انصفناک اکلنا شبیبتک ثم ناخذ منك الجزية ثم كتب الى عماله
Idol worshippers among the Arabs and not upon apostates (13073(94)). And no jizya upon a woman, nor a boy, nor a crippled, nor a blind, nor upon a poor person who is not able to work, nor upon monks who do not mix with people. After understanding Islam, apostasy is even more severe than disbelief; therefore, such a person will be killed in the first instance, or he will embrace Islam. (2) The proof for killing is this hadith: From Ikrimah: 'Ali was brought some zandaqah (heretics) and he burned them. This reached Ibn Abbas, who said: Had I been [in his place], I would not have burned them because the Messenger of Allah forbade punishing with Allah's punishment, and I would have killed them because of the saying of the Messenger of Allah: 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him.' (a) (Sahih al-Bukhari, Chapter on the ruling of apostate men and women… p.1022, no.6922). From this hadith it is known that the apostate will be killed after three days. If the apostate is a woman, she will be imprisoned until she repents. And she will not be left in a state of disbelief. Therefore, jizya will not be taken from her either. Then follows another hadith in Bukhari: 'Then Muadh ibn Jabal followed him… he said: I will not sit until he is killed, the decree of Allah and His Messenger, three times.' (Bukhari, no.6923) That I will not sit until the apostate is killed. (13073(94)) And there is no jizya upon a woman, nor upon a boy, nor upon a crippled, nor upon a blind, nor upon a poor person who does not work, nor upon a monk who does not associate with people. Yes, upon these people jizya is not [incumbent]. These people are poor and not capable of working. Therefore, jizya will not be fixed upon them. (2) The hadith: From Muadh that the Prophet, when he sent him to Yemen, ordered him to take from every halim (i.e., mature adult) a dinar. (b) (Abu Dawood, Chapter on taking jizya, p.74, no.3038). In this hadith, the condition of 'mukhtalim' (having reached puberty) indicates that whoever is not an adult, i.e., a child, has no jizya. And the proof for no jizya on women is this athar: 'Umar ibn al-Khattab wrote to his officials not to impose jizya on women and boys, and not to impose it except on those upon whom the razor has passed.' (c) (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Chapter on increase over the dinar by agreement, vol.9, p.329, no.18683; Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, on what they said about placing jizya and fighting over it, vol.6, p.431, no.32626). From this athar it is known that there is no jizya on women and children. There is no jizya on an old man; the proof is this athar: 'Umar saw a very old dhimmi begging, so he said to him: What is wrong with you? He said: I have no wealth and jizya is taken from me. So Umar said to him: We have not been just to you; we consumed your youth and then we take jizya from you.' Then he wrote to his officials.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proofs from the Kitab al-Siyar explaining which categories of non-Muslims are exempt from jizya. The matn states that jizya is not taken from women, children (boys), the crippled, the blind, a poor person who cannot work, and monks who do not mix with society. The commentator then explains that these individuals are unable to earn or are withdrawn from worldly affairs, so jizya is not imposed on them. For each category, a proof is provided: for women and children, the hadith of Muadh (radiAllahu anhu) mentions taking jizya from every adult (mukhtalim), implying non-adults are exempt, and the athar from Umar (radiAllahu anhu) explicitly excludes women and children. For the elderly, the athar of Umar refusing to take jizya from a very old dhimmi is cited. For the crippled and blind, the commentator uses qiyas (analogy) to the elderly, as they are similarly unable to work. For monks who do not mix with people, the proof is from the treaty of Khalid ibn al-Walid with the people of Hira, which exempted monks who abandoned worldly life. The passage also addresses the case of apostates, though the main focus is on jizya. The Hanafi position is clear: jizya is only obligatory on free, adult, sane, able-bodied men who are not withdrawn from worldly life.

Key Terms
جزیہ (jizya): A poll tax imposed on free, adult, able-bodied non-Muslim subjects (dhimmis) of an Islamic state in exchange for protection and exemption from military service.
زمن (zamin): A crippled or chronically ill person who is unable to work or earn a living.
رهبان (ruhban): Monks who have isolated themselves from worldly life and do not engage in earning a livelihood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, from which of the following is jizya taken?
A) A blind dhimmi
B) A female dhimmi
C) A free adult able-bodied dhimmi male
D) A dhimmi monk who does not work

Key Takeaway
Jizya is only obligatory on free adult able-bodied male dhimmis; all others are exempt based on clear textual proofs.

Jizya Drops Upon Embracing Islam: Ruling and Proof
Ruling with proof: jizya falls upon conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that jizya falls upon a dhimmi's conversion to Islam, cite the hadith proof, and distinguish jizya from kharaj.

Core Idea
Jizya is a humiliation for kufr; once the cause is removed, the obligation ceases entirely.

This passage addresses a crucial scenario: a dhimmi who is liable for jizya converts to Islam. Does the obligation remain? The Hanafi ruling is clear, and it is grounded in both legal reasoning (ta'lil) and a direct hadith. We will work through the ruling, its basis in the concept of humiliation, and the supporting evidence, while noting the important distinction between jizya (personal head tax) and kharaj (land tax).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal ruling
Isolate the specific act: a dhimmi who has jizya upon him (of any kind, past due or current) converts to Islam. The ruling is that jizya falls (سقطت) from him.
This applies to all jizya, including arrears.
2
Understand the rationale (ta'lil)
The commentator explains: jizya is a humiliation (ذلت) due to kufr. After conversion, the person is no longer deserving of that humiliation. Thus the obligation is removed by the very nature of the act.
This is not a cancellation of debt but an automatic removal due to change of legal status.
3
Locate the dalil al-naqli
The hadith of Ibn Abbas quoted by Sufyan: 'There is no jizya on a Muslim' (ليس على مسلم جزية). This is interpreted by Sufyan as applying to a dhimmi who converts.
The hadith is ahad (single) but sound; its meaning is clear and used as the primary proof.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The negation (ليس) indicates absolute absence. The word 'Muslim' is the subject (مسلم). The hadith states that jizya does not exist upon a Muslim. Since the convert is now a Muslim, the jizya is dropped.
The context of the hadith as explained by Sufyan confirms this application.
5
Distinguish jizya from kharaj
If the convert owned land subject to kharaj (land tax), that tax remains because it is tied to the land itself, not the person's religion. This distinction prevents confusion between the two taxes.
Kharaj is a property right of the state, not a humiliation for belief.

پہلے ذمی تھا جس کی وجہ سے اس کے سر پر جزیہ تھا اب وہ مسلمان ہو گیا تو جزیہ ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ البتہ اگر اس کی زمین پر خراج تھا تو وہ

ہو جائے گا۔
3074(95): And whoever embraces Islam while jizya is upon him, it falls from him. 3075(96): And if two years [of jizya] become due on him, they overlap (intervene) into one. Explanation: Previously he was a dhimmi, due to which jizya was upon his head; now he has become Muslim, so jizya will be dropped. However, if there was kharaj (land tax) on his land, it remains. This [jizya] is a thing of humiliation because of kufr, and after becoming Muslim he is not deserving of that humiliation, so it will be dropped. (2) Proof of this is in the hadith: From Ibn Abbas: The Messenger of Allah said: 'There is no jizya upon a Muslim.' Sufyan was asked about its interpretation, and he said: 'When he embraces Islam, there is no jizya upon him.' (c) (Abu Dawood, Chapter on the dhimmi who embraces Islam during some of the years on which jizya is due, p.77, no.3053; Tirmidhi, Chapter on there being no jizya on Muslims, p.138, no.633, Book of Zakat). From this hadith it is known that if a dhimmi becomes Muslim, jizya will be dropped from him.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage contains two successive rulings. The first ruling (95) states that if a dhimmi who was liable for jizya converts to Islam, the jizya obligation falls away. The commentator explains that jizya is a humiliation (dhull) for kufr, and after embracing Islam, the person is no longer deserving of that humiliation. However, if he owns land that was subject to kharaj (land tax), that tax remains because it is attached to the land, not the person's religion. The proof is the hadith of Ibn Abbas (radiAllahu anhu) that the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) said, 'There is no jizya on a Muslim.' Sufyan interpreted this to mean that if a dhimmi converts, jizya is dropped. This ruling applies to both past unpaid jizya and future liability. The second ruling (96, discussed in the next lesson) addresses overlapping years of jizya.

Key Terms
جزية (jizya): Personal head tax imposed on dhimmis (non-Muslims living under Muslim rule) as a mark of humiliation and in exchange for protection.
خراج (kharaj): Land tax on agricultural land owned by dhimmis or in conquered territories; it remains even if the owner converts because it is attached to the land.
سقط (saqata): To fall; used to indicate that an obligation (jizya) falls away and is no longer binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to jizya when a dhimmi converts to Islam?
A) The jizya for the current year must be paid, future years are dropped
B) The jizya is dropped entirely, but kharaj on land remains
C) The jizya must be paid for all past years
D) The jizya is converted into zakat

Key Takeaway
Jizya drops entirely upon embracing Islam because it is a humiliation for disbelief, as confirmed by the hadith 'There is no jizya on a Muslim'.

Overlapping Jizya for Multiple Unpaid Years
Rules on when jizya for two years becomes due and overlaps

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on overlapping jizya for two unpaid years and explain why only one year is due.

Core Idea
Jizya does not accumulate; two unpaid years overlap into one year's obligation.

This passage addresses a situation where a dhimmi fails to pay jizya for two consecutive years. The ruling is unique to the Hanafi school: the two years are said to 'overlap' (tadākhalatā), meaning only one year's payment is taken. This reflects a principle of non-accumulation of annual obligations, easing the burden on the dhimmi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage describes a dhimmi who has not paid jizya for two years. What is the legal situation?
The number of unpaid years is two.
2
Understand the key term
The Arabic term 'tadākhalatā' means they overlap or intervene. Here it indicates that the two obligations merge into one.
This is a specific Hanafi interpretation.
3
Determine the ruling
According to the text, only one year's jizya is obligatory; the other year is dropped.
The ruling is lenient towards the dhimmi.
4
Identify the underlying principle
The Hanafi school treats jizya as an annual obligation that does not accumulate into multiple liabilities when unpaid.
This contrasts with other obligations that may accrue.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Consider if three years are unpaid. Would they also overlap into one? Generally, yes, according to the Hanafi principle.
This is an extension of the same rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۳۰۷۵] (۹۶) وان اجتمع عليه سنتان تداخلتا 
اگر دو سال تک جزیہ نہیں دے سکا تو اب ایک سال کا جزیہ ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ اور ایک سال ہی کا جزیہ لازم ہوگا۔
3075(96): And if two years become due on him, they overlap (intervene) into one. If he could not pay jizya for two years, then the jizya of one year will be dropped, and the jizya of only one year will be obligatory.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This is a concise ruling about a situation where a dhimmi has not paid jizya for two consecutive years. The text says 'suntan tadakhalta' — the two years overlap, meaning that only one year's jizya is taken, not both. The commentator clarifies: if he was unable to pay for two years, the jizya of one year is dropped, and only one year's jizya remains due. This is based on the principle that jizya is an annual obligation that does not multiply into multiple liabilities when not paid on time; rather, the liability is treated as a single continuous obligation that does not accumulate beyond one year. This is a Hanafi position that eases the burden on the dhimmi.

Key Terms
تداخلتا (tadākhalatā): They overlap or intervene, meaning that when two years of jizya are due, the obligation merges into one year's payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a dhimmi fails to pay jizya for two years, how much does he owe?
A) Two years' jizya
B) One year's jizya
C) One and a half years' jizya
D) Nothing, as it is cancelled

Key Takeaway
Jizya does not accumulate; two unpaid years overlap into one year's obligation.

Overlapping Jizya: Hanafi vs Shafi'i/Abu Yusuf
Hanafi ruling on collecting jizya for overlapping years

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on overlapping jizya payments and evaluate the opposing position of Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
When two years' jizya overlap, Hanafis take only one year's payment for the dhimmi's ease.

This lesson examines a practical dispute in jizya collection: what happens when a dhimmi owes jizya for two years simultaneously? The Hanafi school, relying on early caliphal practice and analogy with sadaqat, rules that only one year's jizya is collected. Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Abu Yusuf argue both years must be taken. We'll explore the proofs and the principle of tadakhul (overlapping obligations).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: when two years of jizya overlap, only one year's jizya is taken; the second year's obligation drops.
This is derived from the practice of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman regarding sadaqat and 'ushr.
2
Follow the Hanafi dalil
Examine the narrations from Ta'us and Zuhri that sadaqat are not taken twice in a year, and note the analogy (qiyas) drawn to jizya.
The principle is that overlapping annual obligations are merged, not multiplied.
3
Identify the opposing view
State the position of Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Abu Yusuf: both years' jizya must be collected because the obligations are distinct and combined here.
Their concern is revenue loss and potential evasion by dhimmis.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school prioritizes ease (yusr) for the dhimmi, a core value in Islamic governance, and holds that taking jizya twice in one year contradicts established sunnah.
The thamarah al-khilaf is a practical difference in tax collection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحولان تداخلت الجزيتان [ ۹۷۳۰۶) عن طاؤس انه قال اذا تداركت الصدقات فلا توخذ الاولى كالجزية(الف) (مصنف ابن ابي شيبة ،۱۳۲ من قال لاتو خذ الصدقة في السنة الا مرة واحدة ، ج خانی پس ۴۳۱، نمبر ۱۰۷۳۳ (۲) ایک اور اثر میں ہے۔ عن الزهري قال لم يبلغنا من احد من ولاة هذه الامة الذين كانوا بالمدينة ابوبكر وعمر وعثمان انهم كانوا لا يثنون العشور لكن يبعثون عليها كل عام في الخصب والجدب لان اخذها سنة من رسول الله الا الله (ب) (مصنف ابن ابی شیبة ، من قال لا تو خذ الصدقة في الستة الا مرة واحدة ، ج ثانی، ص ۴۳۱، نمبر ۱۰۷۳۲) اس اثر سے بھی معلوم ہوا کہ ایک سال میں دومرتبہ عشر نہیں لیتے تھے ۔ اور دوسال کا ایک سال میں لیں گے تو دو مرتبہ جزیہ لینا ہوگا۔ اس لئے ایک جزیہ ساقط ہوگا اور ایک جزیہ لازم ہوگا (۳) اس میں ذمی کے لئے سہولت ہے جس میں اسلام میں بڑا خیال رکھا گیا ہے۔ نانه امام شافعی اور امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ دو سال کا جمع ہوا ہے اس لئے دو سال کا جزیہ لیا جائے گا۔ ورنہ حکومت کو نقصان ہوگا اور ذمی ساقط کرنے کے لئے خواہ مخواہ ٹال مٹول کرے گا۔
The two years of jizya overlap [ ۹۷۳۰۶). From Ta'us that he said: When the sadaqat overlap, the first is not taken, like jizya (a) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, 132, chapter: one who says that sadaqa is not taken in the year except once, vol. 2, p. 431, no. 10733) (2) Another narration: from Zuhri that he said: It has not reached us that any of the governors of this nation who were in Madinah – Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman – that they used to take 'ushr twice, but they would send for it every year in times of plenty and drought, because taking it is a sunnah from the Messenger of Allah (b) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, chapter: one who says that sadaqa is not taken in the year except once, vol. 2, p. 431, no. 10732). From this narration it is also known that they did not take 'ushr twice in one year. And if two years are taken in one year then jizya must be taken twice. Therefore one jizya will be dropped and one jizya will be binding (3) In this there is ease for the dhimmi, which is greatly considered in Islam. Indeed Imam Shafi'i and Imam Abu Yusuf say that since two years are combined, jizya for two years will be taken. Otherwise the government will face loss and the dhimmi will unnecessarily do evasion to drop it.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the collection of jizya when two years overlap. The Hanafi position, derived from the practice of the early caliphs and analogy with sadaqat, is that only one year's jizya is taken. The opposing view of Imam Shafi'i and Imam Abu Yusuf is that both years' jizya must be taken to prevent loss to the treasury. The Hanafi response emphasizes ease for the dhimmi, which is an important principle in Islamic governance.

Key Terms
جزیہ (jizyah): A tax paid by non-Muslim subjects (dhimmis) in an Islamic state in return for protection and exemption from military service
تداخل (tadakhul): The overlapping of two obligations due at the same time, leading to one being sufficient

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when two years of jizya payments overlap, how many years' jizya is collected?
A) Two years' jizya
B) Only one year's jizya
C) Three years' jizya
D) No jizya is collected

Key Takeaway
When two years of jizya overlap, the Hanafi school collects only one year's jizya, prioritizing ease for the dhimmi over strict multiplication of obligations.

Requiring Dhimmis to Distinguish Themselves from Muslims
Ruling with proof on dhimmi distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dhimmi distinction and explain the proofs from hadith and the practice of Umar ibn al-Khattab.

Core Idea
Dhimmis must be distinct from Muslims as a binding condition of the dhimma contract.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that dhimmis must visibly distinguish themselves from Muslims in dress, mounts, and other aspects. The passage presents the ruling along with evidence from the Prophet's hadith and the practice of Caliph Umar. Understanding this ruling clarifies the legal nature of the dhimma contract and its social implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
What exactly is required of dhimmis? List the conditions: dress, mounts, not riding horses, not carrying weapons, not initiating greeting, living with humility.
These are conditions of the covenant (عہد), not mere recommendations.
2
Examine the first proof: hadith
The hadith from Abu Huraira prohibits initiating greeting to Jews and Christians and commands forcing them to the narrowest part of the road. This indicates a general requirement of differentiation and humility.
The hadith is from Muslim and Abu Dawud.
3
Examine the second proof: practice of Umar
Umar's letter to army commanders to stamp the necks of dhimmis and his treaty with the people of Sham stipulating distinctiveness in dress, cap, turban, shoes, and speech.
These are aathar (narrations from the Companions) that the Hanafi school relies upon.
4
Determine the legal nature
Are these conditions optional or binding? The passage says a covenant will be taken, implying binding contractual terms. Violation may invalidate the contract.
Hanafis treat these as binding conditions (shurut), not just recommendations.
5
Understand the purpose
The text states it is so that no Muslim greets them or prays for them, and they live with humility to feel inferior and become Muslim quickly.
The purpose combines social distinction and da'wah motivation.

[3078] (99) A covenant will be taken from dhimmis to be distinct from Muslims in dress, riding, [types of] shoes, and caps. And they will not ride horses, nor carry weapons. Dhimmis will be kept in Dar al-Islam, but they must be distinguished from Muslims in every respect, so that no Muslim greets them or prays for them. And they live with humility so that they feel [inferior] and become Muslim quickly. There is a hadith indicating this: from Abu Huraira that the Messenger of Allah said: 'Do not initiate greeting the Jews and Christians, and if you meet any of them on the road, force them to its narrowest part.' (b) (Muslim, chapter: prohibition of initiating greeting to Ahl al-Kitab, p. 213, no. 2167; Abu Dawud, chapter: greeting of dhimmis, p. 360, no. 5205). From this hadith it is understood that one should not initiate greeting a dhimmi, and should tell him to walk on the edge of the road so that his status is not displayed. (2) There is a narration that dhimmis should be stamped so that they can be recognized from a distance. The narration: Umar wrote to the army commanders to stamp the necks of the people of jizya. (c) (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, chapter: imposing conditions that they distinguish between their places and those of Muslims, vol. 9, p. 340, no. 18718). (3) Umar made a treaty with the people of Sham, in which he stipulated that they be distinct in dress, cap, turban, and shoes. Also distinct in speech. The part of the narration: from Abd al-Rahman... Footnote: (b) He said: Do not initiate greeting Jews and Christians. If you meet one of them, force him to the narrow path. (c) Umar wrote to the army commanders: Stamp the necks of the people of jizya.

Commentary
This passage details the requirement that dhimmis must be visibly distinct from Muslims in various aspects of appearance and behavior. The purpose is to maintain the superiority of Islam and avoid any semblance of equality. Proofs include the hadith against initiating greeting and forcing them to the side of the road, Umar's practice of stamping dhimmis, and the peace treaty conditions. These measures encourage dhimmis to embrace Islam and prevent Muslims from honoring them inappropriately.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmī): A non-Muslim subject living under the protection of an Islamic state, paying jizya
تمييز (tamyīz): Distinction or differentiation in appearance and behavior to avoid similarity with Muslims

B) To maintain Islamic superiority and encourage conversion
C) To prevent them from using weapons
D) To facilitate tax collection

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that dhimmis must be distinct from Muslims in appearance and behavior as binding conditions of the dhimma contract, supported by the hadith and the practice of Umar.

Apostate's Property: Inheritance vs Ghanimah
Ruling on division of apostate's property between Muslim heirs and ghanimah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between property acquired by an apostate during Islam and during apostasy, and state the Hanafi ruling for each category with its proof.

Core Idea
Property earned during Islam is inherited by Muslim heirs; property earned during apostasy is ghanimah.

What happens to the property of a person who leaves Islam? The Hanafi school makes a crucial distinction based on when the property was acquired. In this passage, we examine the rule and its evidence from the practice of Sayyiduna Ali (ra) and a Prophetic hadith. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly applying inheritance and spoils rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two periods
Notice that the passage divides the apostate's life into two phases: the period of Islam (حال الإسلام) and the period of apostasy (حال الردة). The ruling changes based on when property was earned.
2
State the ruling for each period
If the apostate dies or is executed, property acquired during Islam goes to his Muslim heirs (ورثته المسلمين). Property acquired during apostasy becomes ghanimah (spoil) for the public treasury.
3
Understand the legal reasoning
Apostasy is treated as a legal death; after death, property is inherited. During apostasy, the apostate is considered a harbi (enemy combatant), so his earnings are like spoils taken from an enemy.
4
Examine the proof
The passage cites an athar of Ali (ra) who killed an apostate and divided his inheritance among Muslim heirs. This is supported by a hadith where the Prophet (saw) ordered execution of an apostate and seizure of his property as ghanimah.
The hadith from Bayhaqi confirms that apostasy-period property is taken as spoils.
5
Recognize the cross-madhhab difference
The Hanafi position differs from the Shafi'i school, which holds that all property of an executed apostate goes to the state treasury, not to Muslim heirs.
This difference affects practical inheritance distribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان مات او قتل على ردّته انتقل ما اكتسبه في حال الاسلام الى ورثته المسلمين وكان ما اكتسبه في حال ردّته فينا [؟] (۱۰۷) اگر وہ مرگیا یا مرتد ہونے کی حالت میں قتل کیا گیا تو اسلام کی حالت میں جو کچھ کمایا وہ اس کے مسلمان ورثہ میں منتقل ہو جائے گا، اور اس کی کمائی مرتد ہونے کی حالت میں غنیمت ہوگی۔ مرتد ہونے کی حالت میں مرگیا یا مرتد ہونے کی حالت میں قتل کیا گیا تو جو کچھ مسلمان ہونے کی حالت میں کمایا تھا وہ اس کے مسلمان ورثہ میں تقسیم ہوگا۔ اور جو کچھ مرتد ہونے کی حالت میں کمایا تھا وہ مال غنیمت شمار ہو گا۔ مسلمان ہونے کی حالت کی کمائی مسلمان ورثہ میں تقسیم ہوگی اس کی دلیل یہ اوپر کے اثر میں گزرگئی۔ عـن علـى ... قال فقتله وجعل ميراثه بين ورثته المسلمين (ب) (مصنف ابن ابی شیبہ نمبر ۳۲۷۵۴) اس اثر میں ہے کہ مرتد کی کمائی مسلمان ورثہ کے درمیان تقسیم ہوگی (۲) مرتد ہونا گویا کہ مرجانا ہے۔ اور مرنے کے بعد اس کا مال ورثہ میں تقسیم ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے اس کا مال بھی ورثہ میں تقسیم ہوگا۔ مرتد کے زمانے میں جو مال کمایا وہ غنیمت ہوگا۔ مرتد ہونے کے بعد وہ حربی ہو گیا اور حربی کا کمایا ہوا مال ہاتھ آجائے تو وہ غنیمت کا مال شمار ہوتا ہے اس لئے مرتد کے زمانے کا مال غنیمت ہو گا (۲) مسئلہ نمبر ۷ ۱۰ میں حدیث گزری کہ سوتیلی ماں سے نکاح کر کے مرتد ہوا تھا تو اس کو قتل کیا اور اس کا مال لے لیا گیا۔ حدیث کا ٹکڑا یہ تھا۔ بعثنی رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم الى رجل نكح امرأة ابيه ان اضرب عنقه و آخذ مالہ (ج) (سن للبیہقی ،نمبر۱۶۸۹۳) جس میں تھا کہ اس کا مال لیکر غنیمت بنالیا جائے۔
And if he dies or is killed in his apostasy, what he acquired during Islam passes to his Muslim heirs, and what he acquired during his apostasy is for the public treasury. (107) If he died or was killed in the state of apostasy, then whatever he earned during Islam will be transferred to his Muslim heirs, and his earnings during apostasy will be ghanimah. If he died in apostasy or was killed in apostasy, then whatever he earned during Islam is divided among his Muslim heirs. And whatever he earned during apostasy is considered ghanimah property. The earnings from the state of Islam are divided among the Muslim heirs; its proof is in the earlier athar. From Ali... he said: So he killed him and made his inheritance among his Muslim heirs. (B) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, no. 32754) In this athar, the apostate's earnings are divided among Muslim heirs. (2) Apostasy is like death. After death, his property is divided among heirs. Therefore his property will also be divided among heirs. Property earned during apostasy becomes ghanimah. After apostasy, he becomes a harbi, and property earned by a harbi that comes into hand is considered ghanimah. Therefore property from the apostasy period is ghanimah. (2) In issue no. 710, the hadith passed that a man married his stepmother and apostatized, so he was killed and his property was taken. The portion of hadith: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) sent me to a man who married his father's wife to strike his neck and take his property. (C) (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, no. 16893) In which it said that his property was taken and made ghanimah.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school regarding the classification of an apostate's property upon his death (by natural causes or execution) while in the state of apostasy. The matn states that property acquired during his Islamic period goes to his Muslim heirs, while property acquired during his apostasy becomes part of the fay' (ghanimah). The commentary expands this: if the apostate dies or is killed in apostasy, his Islamic-period earnings are distributed among his Muslim heirs, and his apostasy-period earnings become ghanimah. The reasoning is that apostasy is treated as legal death; after death, property is inherited. During apostasy, he is considered a harbi (enemy combatant), so his acquisitions are like spoils. The proof is from an athar of Sayyiduna Ali (ra) who killed an apostate and divided his inheritance among Muslim heirs (recorded in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah). A supporting hadith (from Bayhaqi) mentions that the Prophet (saw) ordered the execution of a man who married his stepmother and apostatized, and the taking of his property as ghanimah. This establishes that apostasy-period property is not inherited but becomes public spoils.

Key Terms
ردّة (riddah): Apostasy from Islam; the state of leaving the Islamic faith after having been Muslim.
غنيمت (ghanimah): Spoils of war or property taken from enemy combatants; here, property acquired by the apostate during his apostasy is considered ghanimah when he dies or is executed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, what happens to property acquired by an apostate during the period he was Muslim, if he dies or is executed in apostasy?
A) It becomes ghanimah and is distributed among Muslims.
B) It is divided among his Muslim heirs.
C) It is given to his non-Muslim relatives.
D) It is confiscated by the ruler and used for public works.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between an apostate's earnings before and after apostasy: the former are inherited by his Muslim heirs; the latter become ghanimah.

Apostate flees to dar al-harb: rulings on slaves, debts, and property
Ruling on consequences of ilhaq for an apostate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the legal consequences of an apostate's ilhaq after fleeing to dar al-harb, and explain the proof from the athar.

Core Idea
An apostate who joins dar al-harb legally dies: his mudabbar and umm walad freed, debts due, inheritance divided.

When an apostate flees to enemy territory and the ruler declares him joined (ilhaq), the Hanafi school treats him as dead. This lesson explores the specific consequences—freedom of certain slaves, immediate debt liability, and inheritance transfer—and the proof from the athar of 'Amir and Hakam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
The passage states that the apostate becomes like a dead person (مرده کے درجہ میں). Recognize that ilhaq triggers legal death for all matters.
2
List the three consequences
The mudabbar and umm walad become free; all debts become due immediately; property earned during Islam passes to Muslim heirs.
These consequences mirror actual death.
3
Trace the analogy to actual death
Hanafi scholars reason by qiyas: since ilhaq is treated as death, the rulings of death (such as manumission of mudabbar/umm walad, immediate payment of debts, inheritance) apply.
The analogy is based on the shared 'illa of permanent separation and loss of legal capacity.
4
Examine the athar proof
The athar from 'Amir and Hakam (in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah) explicitly applies 'iddah and inheritance division to an apostate who joins enemy territory. This supports treating him as dead.
The Hanafi school relies on qawl sahabi as a valid proof.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) do not necessarily equate ilhaq with death for all purposes; they may require actual execution or natural death.
This difference affects the timing of freeing slaves and inheritance.

[۱۳۰۸۷ (۱۰۸) پس اگر مرتد ہو کر دار الحرب بھاگ گیا اور حاکم نے اس کے مل جانے کا حکم لگا دیا تو اس کے مدبر اور ام ولد آزاد ہو جائیں گے ۔ اور جو اس پر قرض ہے اس کی ادائیگی فوری ہوگی۔ اور جو کچھ کمایا اسلام کی حالت میں وہ اس کے مسلمان ورثہ کی طرف منتقل ہو جائے گا۔ یہ مسائل اس اصول پر ہیں کہ مرتد دار الحرب بھاگ جائے اور حاکم دارالحرب کے ساتھ مل جانے کا فیصلہ کر دے تو وہ مردہ کے درجہ میں ہو جائے گا۔ اور اس کے تمام معاملات میں مردے کے احکام نافذ ہو جائیں گے۔ مثلا آقا کے مرنے کے بعد مد بر غلام اور ام ولد باندی آزاد ہو جاتے ہیں تو یہاں بھی یہ دونوں آزاد ہو جائیں گے۔ جو قرض کسی متعینہ تاریخ میں ادا کرنا تھا اس کا ابھی ادا کرنا لازم ہوگا کیونکہ آدمی مرنے کے بعد اس کے مال میں فوری قرض ادا کرنالازم ہوتا ہے۔ اور اسلام کی حالت میں جو مال کمایا تھا وہ مسلمان ورثہ میں تقسیم ہو گا کیونکہ مرند گویا کہ مرگیا ہے۔ اثر میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ عن عامر والحكم قالا في الرجل المسلم يرتد عن الاسلام ويلحق بارض العدو فلتعتد امرأته ثلاثة قروء ان كانت تحيض، وان كانت لا تحيض فثلاثة اشهر، وان كانت حاملا ان تضع حملها ويقسم مـيـراثـه بـيـن امرأته وورثته من المسلمين ثم تزوج ان شاءت وان هو رجع فتاب من قبل ان تنقضى عدتها ثبتا على نكاحهما (الف) ( مصنف ابن الى شبيبة ، ۳۱ ما قالوافي المرتد اذ الحق بارض العدد وله امرأة ما حالها ، ج سادس جس ۲۴۵، نمبر ۵۲ ۳۲۷) اس اثر میں ہے کہ دارالحرب چلا جائے تو اس کی بیوی بائنہ ہو جائے گی اور اس کا مال ورثہ میں تقسیم ہو جائے گا۔ انمول مرتد ہو کر دار الحرب میں مل جائے تو وہ مردہ کے درجے میں ہو جاتا ہے۔
(108) So if an apostate flees to dar al-harb and the ruler pronounces his joining (ilhaq), then his mudabbar and umm walad become free. And the debts upon him become due immediately. And whatever he earned during Islam transfers to his Muslim heirs. These issues are based on the principle that if an apostate flees to dar al-harb and the ruler decides that he has joined the enemy, then he becomes like a dead person. And the rulings of a dead person are implemented in all his matters. For example, after the death of the master, the mudabbar slave and umm walad become free; here also both become free. A debt that was to be paid on a specific date now must be paid immediately because after a person's death, it is obligatory to pay debts from his property immediately. And the property he earned during Islam is divided among his Muslim heirs because the apostate is as if dead. There is an athar providing evidence: From 'Amir and Hakam, they said regarding a Muslim man who apostatized and joined the enemy land: then his wife shall observe 'iddah for three menstrual cycles if she menstruates, and if she does not menstruate then three months, and if she is pregnant then until she delivers. And his inheritance is divided among his wife and his Muslim heirs. Then she may marry if she wishes. And if he returns and repents before her 'iddah expires, they remain on their marriage. (A) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, ...) In this athar, it is stated that if he goes to dar al-harb, his wife becomes ba'in and his property is divided among the heirs. In short, when an apostate joins dar al-harb, he becomes like a dead person.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling regarding an apostate who flees to dar al-harb (enemy territory) and the Muslim ruler pronounces a judgment of ilhaq (joining the enemy). The Hanafi position is that such an apostate is legally treated as dead. Consequently: (1) His mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon the master's death) and umm walad (a slave concubine who bore a child to her master) are freed, as they would be upon the master's actual death. (2) All his debts become immediately due, regardless of original terms, because debts are expedited upon death in Islam. (3) His property acquired during his Islamic period passes to his Muslim heirs, as it would from a deceased Muslim. This ruling is analogized from the case of actual death (qiyas). The proof is an athar from 'Amir and Hakam, recorded in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, which states that if a Muslim apostatizes and joins the enemy land, his wife must observe 'iddah, his inheritance is divided among his wife and Muslim heirs, and if he returns before her 'iddah expires, the marriage is revived. This athar supports treating the apostate as dead for the purposes of inheritance and marital dissolution.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose master has declared that he will be free upon the master's death. Here, if the apostate master is declared dead by ilhaq, the mudabbar is freed.
ام ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master. Upon the master's death, she becomes free. Here, she is freed upon the apostate master's ilhaq-death.
الحاق (ilhaq): The legal declaration by a Muslim ruler that an apostate has joined enemy territory, thereby treating him as dead for certain rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal effect of the ruler's declaration of ilhaq on an apostate who has fled to dar al-harb?
A) He remains a Muslim and his property is untouched.
B) He is treated as dead, freeing his mudabbar and umm walad, making debts due, and dividing Islamic-period property among Muslim heirs.
C) His wife must immediately remarry.
D) His debts are forgiven because he is considered a martyr.

Key Takeaway
Ilhaq legally transforms an apostate in dar al-harb into a dead person, activating all rulings of death regarding slaves, debts, and inheritance.

Allocation of an Apostate's Debts: Islamic vs. Apostasy Period
Rule and reasoning for segregating apostate's debts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the segregation of an apostate's debts based on the period of incurrence and explain the legal reasoning (ta'lil) that distinguishes the two estates.

Core Idea
Apostasy divides an apostate's property: debts from Islam paid from Islamic earnings, debts from apostasy from apostasy earnings.

The Hanafi school treats apostasy as a legal death, but with a suspension pending possible return. This creates two distinct financial periods: before and after apostasy. In this lesson, we examine how debts incurred in each period are allocated to the corresponding property, ensuring creditors and heirs are justly served.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of debt
The passage divides debts into two types: those incurred while the person was Muslim (pre-apostasy) and those incurred after apostasy. Recognize each category.
Debt incurred during the Islamic period is called 'qard fi al-Islam'; during apostasy, 'qard fi al-riddah'.
2
State the allocation rule
Each debt is paid from property earned in the same period: Islamic debts from Islamic earnings, apostasy debts from apostasy earnings. This is the core ruling.
The ruling is explicit: 'The debt that became obligatory during Islam is paid from what he earned during Islam.'
3
Understand the underlying principle
The ruling is based on the principle that apostasy is akin to legal death from the moment it occurs, but with a suspended status (mawquf) in hope of return. If the apostate does not return, he is considered dead from the time of apostasy.
This creates two separate legal personalities: the Muslim and the apostate, each with its own estate.
4
Apply the principle to justify the rule
Since apostasy is treated as death, the pre-apostasy property belongs to the Muslim personality (to be inherited after debts), and the post-apostasy property belongs to the apostate personality (ultimately becoming ghanimah). Debts follow their respective personalities.
This prevents mixing estates and ensures each period's creditors are satisfied from the appropriate assets.
5
Note the cross-madhhab implication
Some other schools may not recognize this segregation and treat all assets as one estate for debt payment. Understand that the Hanafi view is mukhtalaf fih.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that in the Hanafi school, heirs may inherit Islamic property after debts, while apostasy property is handled by the state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۳۰۸۸ (۱۰۹) وہ قرض جو اسلام کی حالت میں لازم ہوا ہے ادا کیا جائے اس سے جو اسلام کی حالت میں کمایا۔ اور وہ قرض جو لازم ہوا ہے مرتد کی حالت میں اس سے ادا کیا جائے جو مرتد کی حالت میں کمایا۔ ر یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ مرتد ہوتے ہی گویا کہ مر گیا۔ البتہ دوبارہ مسلمان ہونے کی امید پر اس کا معاملہ موقوف رکھا گیا۔ جب دو بارہ مسلمان نہیں ہوا تو مرتد ہونے کے وقت ہی سے مردہ شمار کیا جائے گا۔ اس لئے مرنے سے پہلے یعنی اسلام کی حالت میں جو قرض لیا تھا وہ اسلام کی حالت میں جو مال کمایا تھا اس سے ادا کیا جائے گا۔ اور مرنے کے بعد یعنی مرتد ہونے کے بعد جو قرض لیا اس کو مرتد ہونے کے بعد کمائے ہوئے مال سے ادا کیا جائے گا۔
(109) The debt that became obligatory during Islam is paid from what he earned during Islam. And the debt that became obligatory during the apostate state is paid from what he earned during the apostate state. This issue is based on the principle that as soon as he apostatized, it is as if he died. However, his affair was suspended in hope of his returning to Islam. When he did not return to Islam, he is considered dead from the time of apostasy. Therefore, the debt he took before death, i.e., in the state of Islam, will be paid from the property he earned in Islam. And the debt he took after death, i.e., after apostasy, will be paid from the property he earned after apostasy.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This lesson provides a subsidiary ruling on the allocation of debts from an apostate's two estates. The Hanafi school distinguishes between debts incurred during the Islamic period and those incurred during apostasy. Debts from the Islamic period are paid from property acquired during Islam, while debts from the apostasy period are paid from property acquired during apostasy. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that apostasy is treated as legal death from the moment it occurs, but with a suspended status (mawquf) pending possible return to Islam. If the apostate does not repent (and thus is executed or flees), the suspension is lifted, and he is regarded as dead from the moment of apostasy. Consequently, his life is effectively divided into two periods: before apostasy (Islamic period) and after apostasy (apostasy period). Debts are attributed to the period in which they were incurred. This prevents mixing the two estates, as the apostasy-period property is ultimately destined to become ghanimah, not inheritance, while Islamic-period property goes to heirs after debts are paid.

Key Terms
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; the apostate's status is temporarily held in abeyance pending his possible repentance. Until resolved, it is as if he is between life and death.
قرض (qard): Debt; a financial obligation incurred by the apostate before or after apostasy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, how are debts of an apostate paid if he has property from both his Islamic period and his apostasy period?
A) All debts are paid from the Islamic-period property first.
B) All debts are paid from the apostasy-period property first.
C) Debts from the Islamic period are paid from Islamic property; debts from the apostasy period are paid from apostasy property.
D) The debts are pooled and paid proportionally from both estates.

Key Takeaway
Debts are attributed to the period in which they were incurred, matching the estate's source.

Procedure for Dealing with Muslim Rebels (Bughat)
Ruling with proof on rebel procedure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedure for dealing with Muslim rebels and derive the ruling from the Quranic verse and hadith.

Core Idea
Rebels must be called back and doubts removed before any fighting; war only if they attack and refuse return.

The passage outlines the imam's obligations when a Muslim group rebels and seizes a city. The Hanafi school emphasizes reconciliation and dialogue before any use of force, based on the Quranic command and prophetic example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and its Ruling
The imam's duty upon hearing of a rebellion: call the rebels back to the community and remove their doubts. This is wajib (obligatory). Fighting is only permitted if the rebels initiate hostilities and persist after being called.
2
Locate the Dalil
The Quranic verse (49:9) commands fighting only against the transgressing party until they return to Allah's command, implying prior dialogue. Additionally, a hadith from Muslim orders striking anyone who divides the united ummah.
Quran verse is quoted in full; hadith is referenced but not in full.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The verse 'fight against the one that transgresses until it returns to the command of Allah' indicates that fighting is conditional on transgression (baghy) and persistence. The hadith shows that dividing the community is a severe offense, but the passage stresses removing doubts first, as shown by Ibn Abbas's mission.
The Hanafi understanding is that verbal clarification precedes combat.
4
Note the Historical Application
The commentary cites Caliph Ali sending Ibn Abbas to the Kharijites (Haruriyyah) to remove their three doubts. This illustrates the practical implementation of the ruling.
The doubts were doctrinal; Ibn Abbas gave satisfactory answers, though some remained.
5
Summarize the Hierarchical Approach
The imam must follow this sequence: 1) Call rebels to the community, 2) Remove their doubts, 3) If they start fighting, fight until they return, 4) Once they return, make peace with justice.
This is the mufta bihi position from zahir al-riwayah.

Sustenance of the warriors and their children. [13094 (115)] And if a group of Muslims gains control over a city and leaves the obedience of the imam, he should call them back to the community and remove their doubts, and he should not initiate fighting until they initiate it. Rulings on rebels: [3094] (115) If a group of Muslims dominate a city and leave the imam's obedience, then call them to return to the community, and remove their doubt, and as long as they do not start fighting, we will not fight them. A group of Muslims opposes the imam and leaves his obedience and seizes a city, they will be invited to join the community. If there is any doubt in joining the community, it will be removed. And because they are Muslims, as long as they do not start war with us, we will not fight them. There is an indication in the verse: 'And if two parties among the believers fight, then make peace between them. But if one transgresses against the other, then fight against the one that transgresses until it returns to the command of Allah. Then if it returns, make peace between them with justice, and be fair. Indeed Allah loves those who are fair.' (Surah Al-Hujurat, verse 9) From this verse it is learned that if a rebel group starts fighting, then you should fight until they submit. So if they submit, stop fighting and do reconciliation and justice. This shows that if they fight, we will fight; if they surrender, we stop fighting. (2) In hadith: I heard 'Arfajah say: I heard the Messenger of Allah (PBUH) say: 'Indeed there will be trials and turmoils. Whoever wants to cause division in the affairs of this ummah while it is united, strike him with the sword, whoever he is.' (Muslim, Bab hukm man farraqa amr al-muslimin wa huwa mujtami', p.128 no.1852) From this hadith it is learned that if any group tries to disperse the Muslims, they should be fought. Efforts will be made to remove their doubts. When the Haruriyyah group rebelled against Hadrat Ali, Hadrat Abdullah ibn Abbas went to convince them and remove their doubts. They had three doubts, to which he gave satisfactory answers. A portion of the long hadith: Abdullah ibn Abbas narrated: When the Haruriyyah came out, they gathered in a house, six thousand in number. I came to Ali and said: O Commander of the Believers, delay the afternoon prayer so that I may go to these people and speak to them. Annotation (a): If two parties of believers fight, make peace between them. Then if one transgresses against the other, fight the transgressor until it returns to Allah's command. Then if it returns, make peace between them with justice and be fair. Indeed Allah loves those who are fair. (b) He (PBUH) said: There will be dangerous situations; so whoever divides the community while it is united, kill him with the sword, whoever he may be.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the procedure for dealing with Muslim rebels (bughat). The Hanafi ruling: if a group of Muslims rebels against the imam and seizes a city, the imam must first call them back to the community and attempt to remove their doubts (shubuhāt) through dialogue. Fighting is only allowed if the rebels initiate hostilities. This is based on Quran 49:9, which commands fighting only against the transgressing party until they return to Allah's command, and then making peace with justice. The hadith from Muslim warns against anyone who tries to divide the Muslim community, ordering them to be struck with the sword. The commentary further illustrates this with the historical example of the Haruriyyah (Kharijites) during Caliph Ali's rule, where Ibn Abbas was sent to address their misconceptions. The key principle is the priority of dialogue and reconciliation over combat when dealing with Muslim rebels.

Key Terms
بغی (baghy): Rebellion against the legitimate imam by a Muslim group with a ta'wil (interpretation).
شبہ (shubha): Doubt or misunderstanding that leads to rebellion; must be addressed before force.
حروریہ (haruriyyah): An early Kharijite group that rebelled against Caliph Ali.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Immediately upon their rebellion
B) Only after they attack first
C) Only after they refuse to return to the community and persist in fighting
D) Never, because they are Muslims

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school obligates dialogue and removal of doubts before any military action against Muslim rebels, with fighting only as a last resort.

Rulings on Muslim Rebels: Invitation, Engagement, and Post-Battle Treatment
Rulings on Muslim rebels: invitation, fighting, and post-battle treatment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedure for dealing with Muslim rebels (bughat), including the obligation to invite and remove doubts, the condition for initiating combat, and the rules for treating wounded and fugitives based on the presence of a fata.

Core Idea
Before any sword is drawn, the imam must extend a hand of guidance; after battle, mercy depends on the enemy's strength.

Scholars have detailed how an Islamic state should handle armed rebellion by fellow Muslims. This passage from Kitab al-Siyar outlines the Hanafi position: the imam must first invite rebels back and address their grievances. Only if they initiate force does fighting become obligatory. Even then, post-battle treatment varies—whether wounded are finished off and fugitives pursued depends on whether the rebels have a strong faction (fata) to which they can regroup. Understanding these rules ensures that the response to rebellion remains just and avoids unnecessary bloodshed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial obligation
The imam must call the rebels to return to the community (da'wa) and remove their doubts (raf' al-shubha). Fighting is not to begin until they start it.
The text says 'لا يبدؤهم بالقتال حتى يبدؤه' – he does not fight them until they begin.
2
Recognize the condition for combat
If the rebels initiate fighting, then it becomes wajib to fight them until their group is broken. The Quranic imperative 'قاتلوا التي تبغي' indicates obligation.
Surah Al-Hujurat 49:9 commands fighting the transgressing party until they return to Allah's command.
3
Understand the proofs (dalil)
The Quran (49:9) and hadith (Arfajah and Ibn Abbas narrations) are cited. The hadith stresses striking anyone who tries to split the ummah. The practice of Ali with the Haruriyya illustrates the removal of doubts.
The passage also cites the Athar of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz to show that fighting is only when rebels initiate or cause corruption.
4
Distinguish post-battle rules based on fata
If the rebels have a large faction (fata) to which they can return, their wounded may be finished off (i.e., not taken prisoner) and fugitives pursued. If they have no fata, this is not done because they are already scattered.
The text explicitly: 'وان كانت لهم فئة أجهز على جريحهم وأتبع مُوَلّيهم وان لم يكن لهم فئة لـم يـجهـز عـلـى جريحهم ولم يُتبع'
5
Reflect on the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
This distinction prevents excessive force when the rebels are already neutralized. It also aligns with the principle that the goal is to end rebellion, not to exterminate.
The Hanafi position differs from some schools that may permit more aggressive post-battle action regardless of fata.

وابت طائفة منهم ان يرجعوا فارسل عمر رجلا على خيل وامره ان ينزل حيث يرحلون ولا يحركهم ولا يهيجهم، فان قتلوا وافسدوا في الارض فاسط عليهم وقاتلهم وان هم لم يقتلوا ولم يفسدوا في الارض فدعهم

حاشیہ : (الف) عبد اللہ بن عباس فرماتے ہیں کہ جب حروریہ کے لوگوں نے خروج کیا تو وہ ایک گھر میں جمع ہوئے۔ وہ اس وقت چھ ہزار تھے۔ میں حضرت علی کے پاس آیا اور کہا اے امیر المومنین ! ظہر ٹھنڈا کر کے پڑھئے ۔ میں ان لوگوں سے جا کر بات کرتا ہوں . حضرت عبد اللہ بن عباس فرماتے ہیں کہ قوم میں سے دو ہزار رجوع کر گئے اور باقی گمراہی پر قتل کئے گئے (ب) حضرت عمر بن عبد العزیز نے خوارج سے جھگڑا کیا۔ ان میں سے کچھ لوٹا اور ایک جماعت لوٹنے سے انکار کرگئی۔ تو حضرت عمر نے ایک آدمی کو گھوڑے پر بھیجا اور اس کو حکم دیا کہ جہاں وہ ٹھہرتے ہیں وہاں اتریں۔ اور ان کو بھڑکائے نہیں۔ پس اگر انہوں نے قتال کیا اور زمین میں فساد برپا کیا تو اس پر مسلط ہو جائیں اور ان سے قتال کریں۔ اور اگر انہوں نے قتال نہیں کیا اور زمین میں فساد برپا نہیں کیا تو ان کو چھوڑ دیں، جانے دیں۔
Book of Siyar. Provisions of fighters and their dependents. [3094] (115) And if a group of Muslims overcomes a city and leaves the obedience of the imam, he calls them to return to the community and removes their doubts, and he does not begin fighting them until they begin it. Rulings on rebels. [3094] (115) If a group of Muslims dominates a city and leaves the obedience of the imam, he invites them to return to the community, removes their doubts, and as long as they do not start fighting, we do not fight them. There is an indication in the verse: 'And if two parties of the believers fight, make peace between them. Then if one of them transgresses against the other, fight the one that transgresses until it returns to the command of Allah. Then if it returns, make peace between them with justice and act equitably. Indeed, Allah loves those who act equitably.' (Surah Al-Hujurat: 9) This verse indicates that if a rebel group starts fighting, you fight until they submit. If they submit, stop fighting and make peace. Also a hadith: 'I heard Arfajah say: I heard the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) say: There will be tribulations. Whoever tries to divide this ummah when it is united, strike him with the sword, whoever he is.' (Muslim) This hadith shows that if a group tries to disperse Muslims, they are fought. Their doubts are to be removed. The story of Ali and the Haruriyya: Ibn Abbas went to convince them and removed their three doubts. The longer narration: 'Abdullah ibn Abbas said: When the Haruriyya revolted, they gathered in a house, six thousand strong. I came to Ali and said, O Commander of the Faithful, delay the noon prayer so that I may go and speak to these people.' Footnote (a): If two groups of believers fight, make peace. If one transgresses, fight the transgressor until they return to Allah's command. If they return, make peace with justice. Allah loves the just. (b) The Prophet said: There will be dangerous situations. Whoever tries to split the ummah while it is united, strike him with the sword, whoever he is. [3095] (116) So if they begin, fight them until their group is broken. And if they have a group (faction), then finish off their wounded and pursue their fugitives. And if they do not have a group, do not finish off their wounded and do not pursue their fugitives. 'Abdullah ibn Abbas said: Two thousand from the group returned, and the rest were killed in error. (Mustadrak al-Hakim) This hadith indicates that if a rebel group has doubts, they are advised. We do not start fighting because they are Muslims. However, if we see that they are fully prepared and not fighting will make the matter difficult, then it is permissible to eliminate them first. [3095] (116) So if they initiate, fight them until their party is broken. If they have a large group, capture their wounded and pursue their fugitives. If they do not have a large group, do not capture their wounded and do not pursue their fugitives. The proof for this is the Athar: 'Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz debated with the Khawarij. Some returned, but a faction refused to return. He sent a man on a horse with orders to alight where they alight and not to provoke them. If they kill and cause corruption on earth, then attack and fight them. If they do not kill and do not cause corruption, leave them to travel.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah) This Athar shows that fighting is only permissible if they start fighting and cause corruption. Otherwise, they are left alone. The hadith from Ibn Umar is also proof that if the rebels have no group, their wounded are not captured and their fugitives are not pursued.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Siyar deals with the Ahkam al-Bughat (rulings on rebels). It is a ruling_with_proof passage. The author first states the general rule: if a Muslim group rebels against the imam, they must first be invited to return to the community and their doubts must be clarified. Fighting is not initiated against them until they start it. This is based on the Quranic verse 49:9, which commands fighting only against the transgressing party, and the hadith of Arfajah, which orders striking anyone who tries to split the Muslim community. The example of Ali sending Ibn Abbas to the Khawarij illustrates the method of removing doubts. Then the passage discusses what happens if they do initiate fighting: they are fought until their group is broken. However, the treatment of wounded and fugitives differs depending on whether they have a strong faction (fata) to which they can return. If they have a large group, wounded may be finished off (i.e., not taken prisoner) and fugitives pursued to prevent regrouping. If they have no significant group, wounded are not finished off and fugitives are not pursued because they are already scattered. The proof for this distinction is the action of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz with the Khawarij, as recorded by Ibn Abi Shaybah. The Hanafi position is clear: the imam must attempt reconciliation first, and fighting is only permissible when the rebels initiate it. The level of force after battle depends on the rebels' remaining strength.

Key Terms
بغي (baghy): Transgression or rebellion against the legitimate imam without justification, leading to the status of bughat (rebels).
فئة (fata): A group or faction to which the rebels can retreat and regroup; their existence affects the permissibility of finishing off wounded and pursuing fugitives.
شبهة (shubha): Doubt or ambiguity that leads to rebellion; must be removed before fighting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rulings in this passage, when is it permissible to finish off the wounded of the rebels?
A) Always, because they are rebels.
B) Only if they have a large group (fata) to which they can return.
C) Only if they have no group.
D) Never, as it is always prohibited.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school mandates invitation and doubt removal before fighting rebels, and post-battle treatment depends on whether the rebels retain a viable faction.

Ruling on Pursuing Rebels: When to Kill Wounded and Chase Fleeing
Conditional ruling on pursuit and killing of rebel wounded based on existence of a fiah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pursuing fleeing rebels and finishing wounded rebels, and identify the condition (existence of a fiah) that determines the ruling.

Core Idea
Only rebels with enough strength to regroup are pursued; isolated rebels are left alone.

This passage from Kitab al-Siyar addresses a specific question: how should the Muslim army treat rebels who have been defeated? The answer depends on whether the rebels still have a cohesive group capable of regrouping. The Hanafi school distinguishes between rebels with a fiah (substantial group) and those without. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its conditional ruling
The passage gives rulings for three acts: fighting rebels who initiate, killing their wounded, and pursuing their fleeing. Each is conditional on the rebels having a fiah.
The base ruling is that only rebels who start the war are fought.
2
Locate the condition
The key condition is whether the rebels have a fiah (group/faction) capable of regrouping. If they do, harsh measures are allowed; if not, they are not.
The term fiah implies a sizeable, organized group.
3
Understand the wisdom behind the condition
Rebels with a fiah pose a continued threat and can reorganize to cause further fitna. Those without are scattered and harmless.
The aim is to prevent future rebellion, not to punish.
4
Examine the dalil
The proof cited is the practice of Umar bin Abdul Aziz with the Khawarij: he ordered not to fight unless they initiate violence and corruption. This supports the conditional approach.
The athar is considered a strong precedent (athar) in Hanafi usul.
5
Note cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school permits pursuing and killing wounded regardless of the existence of a fiah. The Hanafi conditional approach is more restrictive.
This difference stems from varying views on the status of rebels as Muslims.

ان سے قتال کرے۔ یہاں تک کہ ان کا جتھا ٹوٹ جائے۔ اور اگر ان کی جماعت بھی ہو تو گرفتار کرے ان کے زخمیوں کو، اور تعاقب کرے ان کے بھاگنے والوں کا۔ اور اگر ان کی جمعیت نہ ہو تو نہ گرفتار کرے ان کے زخمیوں کو اور نہ تعاقب کرے بھاگنے والوں کا۔ باغی جماعت ہم سے جنگ شروع کر دیں تو اب ان سے قتال کیا جائے گا اور اتنا قتال کیا جائے گا کہ ان کی جمعیت ٹوٹ جائے ۔ پس اگر کوئی اچھی خاصی جماعت ہو تو ان کے زخمیوں کو قید کرے اور ان کے بھاگنے والوں کا پیچھا کرے تا کہ وہ بدحواس ہو کر دوبارہ جمع ہونے کی کوشش نہ کریں۔ اور جن کی کوئی اچھی خاصی جماعت نہیں ہے اس کے زخمیوں کو قید نہ کرے اور نہ بھاگنے والوں کا پیچھا کرے۔ کیونکہ اس کی جماعت نہیں ہے تو یوں بھی وہ منتشر ہو گئے۔ وہ جنگ کی ابتدا کریں تب ہم جنگ کریں اس کی دلیل یہ اثر ہے۔ خاصم عمر بن عبد العزيز الخوارج فرجع من رجع منهم وابت طائفة منهم ان يرجعوا فارسل عمر رجلا على خيل وامره ان ينزل حيث يرحلون ولا يحركهم ولا يهيجهم، فان قتلوا وافسدوا في الارض فاسط عليهم وقاتلهم وان هم لم يقتلوا ولم يفسدوا في الارض فدعهم يسيرون (ب) (مصنف ابن ابی هیبة ، ۳ ماذکر فی الخوارج، ج سابع ص ۵۵۶ نمبر ۳۷۸۹۵) اس اثر میں ہے کہ وہ قتال کرے اور زمین میں فساد برپا کرے تو قتال کیا جائے۔ اور اگر قتال نہ کرے تو ان کو زمین میں گھومنے دیا جائے۔ اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ قتال شروع کرے تب ہی اس سے جنگ کی جائے ورنہ نہیں۔ باغی کی جماعت نہ ہو تو اس کے زخمی کوقید نہ کیا جائے اور اس کے بھاگنے والے کا پیچھا نہ کیا جائے اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن ابن عمر حاشیہ : (الف) عبد اللہ بن عباس فرماتے ہیں کہ جب حروریہ کے لوگوں نے خروج کیا تو وہ ایک گھر میں جمع ہوئے۔ وہ اس وقت چھ ہزار تھے۔ میں حضرت علی کے پاس آیا اور کہا اے امیر المومنین ! ظہر ٹھنڈا کر کے پڑھئے ۔ میں ان لوگوں سے جا کر بات کرتا ہوں . حضرت عبد اللہ بن عباس فرماتے ہیں کہ قوم میں سے دو ہزار رجوع کر گئے اور باقی گمراہی پر قتل کئے گئے (ب) حضرت عمر بن عبد العزیز نے خوارج سے جھگڑا کیا۔ ان میں سے کچھ لوٹا اور ایک جماعت لوٹنے سے انکار کرگئی۔ تو حضرت عمر نے ایک آدمی کو گھوڑے پر بھیجا اور اس کو حکم دیا کہ جہاں وہ ٹھہرتے ہیں وہاں اتریں۔ اور ان کو بھڑکائے نہیں۔ پس اگر انہوں نے قتال کیا اور زمین میں فساد برپا کیا تو اس پر مسلط ہو جائیں اور ان سے قتال کریں۔ اور اگر انہوں نے قتال نہیں کیا اور زمین میں فساد برپا نہیں کیا تو ان کو چھوڑ دیں، جانے دیں۔
Book of Siyar [3095 (11)] If they initiate, fight them until their group is dispersed. And if they have a faction, finish off their wounded and pursue their fleeing ones. And if they have no faction, do not finish off their wounded and do not pursue them. Abdullah bin Abbas said: Two thousand of the people returned and the rest were killed in misguidance. (a) (Mustadrak al-Hakim, Kitab Ahl al-Baghi, which is the end of Jihad, vol. 2, p. 164, no. 2656) In this hadith, if the rebel group is confused, they should be counseled. We will not start fighting first because they are also Muslims. However, if we see they are fully prepared and fighting is not done, the matter will become difficult. In such a situation, it is permissible to suppress them first. [3095] (116) So if they initiate, fight them until their group is broken. And if they have a group, arrest their wounded and pursue their fleeing ones. And if they have no group, do not arrest their wounded and do not pursue their fleeing ones. When the rebel group starts war with us, then we fight them, and we fight until their group is broken. So if they have a sizable group, he should arrest their wounded and pursue their fleeing ones so that they do not become panicked and try to regroup. And those who do not have a sizable group, he should not arrest their wounded nor pursue their fleeing ones because they are already scattered. They initiate war, then we fight. The proof for this is this athar: Umar bin Abdul Aziz disputed with the Khawarij. Some of them returned, and a group refused to return. So Umar sent a man on horseback and ordered him to dismount where they camp, not to move them nor provoke them. If they kill and cause corruption in the land, then attack them and fight them. And if they do not kill and do not cause corruption in the land, then leave them to travel. (b) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, 3 what is mentioned about the Khawarij, vol. 7, p. 556, no. 37895) In this athar, if they fight and cause corruption in the land, then fight them. And if they do not fight, let them roam the land. From this it is learned that only when they start fighting should they be fought, otherwise not. If the rebel has no group, his wounded should not be arrested and his fleeing should not be pursued. The proof for this is this hadith: from Ibn Umar. Footnote: (a) Abdullah bin Abbas says: When the Haruriyya rebelled, they gathered in a house. At that time they were six thousand. I came to Ali and said, O Commander of the Faithful! Delay the Zuhr prayer until it cools. I will go and speak to these people. Abdullah bin Abbas says: Two thousand of the people returned and the rest were killed in misguidance. (b) Umar bin Abdul Aziz disputed with the Khawarij. Some of them returned and a group refused to return. So Umar sent a man on horseback and ordered him to dismount where they camp, not to move them nor provoke them. So if they fought and caused corruption in the land, then attack them and fight them. And if they did not fight and did not cause corruption in the land, then leave them, let them go.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn of Kitab al-Siyar regarding the treatment of rebels (baghi) who take up arms against the Muslim community. The ruling distinguishes between rebel groups that have a substantial following (fiah/group) and those that are isolated. If rebels initiate war, the Muslim ruler must fight them until their group is scattered. If they have a group, their wounded may be killed (to prevent them from rejoining the fight) and their fleeing members may be pursued (to prevent them from regrouping). However, if they have no group, the wounded should not be killed and the fleeing should not be pursued, as they pose no further threat. The commentary elaborates that rebels are Muslims, so fighting them is only permissible if they start war. The proof cited is the practice of Umar bin Abdul Aziz with the Khawarij: he ordered not to fight them unless they initiated violence and corruption. The footnotes provide additional evidence: the incident of Abdullah bin Abbas convincing two thousand Khawarij to return, and the practice of Ali during the Battle of Jamal not to pursue fleeing or kill wounded.

Key Terms
فئة (fiah): A group or faction with enough strength to regroup and continue fighting
جريح (jarih): Wounded person; in this context, a rebel wounded in battle
مولي (muwalli): One who turns his back, i.e., fleeing from battle

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, when is it permissible to pursue fleeing rebels?
A) Always, because they are rebels
B) Only if they have a group (fiah) that could regroup
C) Never, because they are Muslims
D) Only if they have killed someone

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on pursuing rebels and finishing wounded is conditional upon the rebels having a fiah capable of regrouping; without it, they must be left alone.

Rebel Offspring and Property Are Protected; Permissibility of Using Their Weapons
Ruling with proof from the practice of Ali (RA).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the captivity and property of rebels and explain the evidence from the practice of Ali (RA).

Core Idea
Rebels remain Muslims; their offspring cannot be enslaved nor property divided as ghanimah.

This lesson examines two interconnected rulings from the matn: the prohibition of taking rebel offspring captive and dividing their property, and the permissibility of using their weapons if needed. The proof comes from the practice of Caliph Ali during the Battle of Basra, which established that rebels are to be treated as Muslims with protected rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the Arabic text: the first ruling forbids capturing offspring and dividing property; the second permits using rebels' weapons when Muslims need them.
Note the logical link: both rulings stem from the fact that rebels are Muslims.
2
Understand the legal basis
Rebels are not non-Muslims; thus the usual rules of jihad (enslavement, ghanimah) do not apply. Their Islam protects their lives, property, and offspring.
This differs from the treatment of non-Muslim combatants.
3
Examine the dalil
The text cites the hadith of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and the athar of Ali (RA). In the battle of Basra, Ali prohibited pursuing the fleeing, killing prisoners, finishing the wounded, and taking any belongings. Additionally, when asked about taking offspring captive, he replied: 'There is no captivity for them; we only fought those who fought us.'
These reports establish the general principle of non-aggression beyond active combatants.
4
Note the qualification
The commentary adds that if the rebels have a strong group behind them, it is permissible to pursue and capture to prevent fitna. But this does not change the inviolability of their offspring and property.
This exception is about detaining or pursuing, not about enslavement or spoliation.
5
Apply to modern contexts
Consider a modern civil conflict between Muslim groups. The Hanafi ruling prohibits treating the opponent's family as captives or their property as booty, even if fighting is justified.
This protects the fundamental rights of Muslims even in rebellion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[3096] (117) And their offspring are not to be taken captive, and their property is not to be divided. [3097] (118) And there is no harm if they fight with their weapons if the Muslims need them. He said: The Messenger of Allah said to Abdullah ibn Mas'ud: O Ibn Mas'ud, do you know what is Allah's judgment regarding those who rebel from this Ummah? Ibn Mas'ud said: Allah and His Messenger know best. He said: Allah's judgment regarding them is that their fleeing ones should not be pursued, their prisoners should not be killed, and their wounded should not be finished off. (a) (Mustadrak al-Hakim, Kitab Ahl al-Baghi, vol. 2, p. 168, no. 2662; Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Bab Ahl al-Baghi, vol. 8, p. 1314, no. 19747) In this hadith, the fleeing of the rebel should not be pursued, nor their prisoner killed, nor their wounded killed because they are Muslims. And if he has a group behind him, then arrest his prisoner and pursue his fleeing ones because these people will join the group and spread more mischief. And if that is not expected, then do not arrest prisoners and do not pursue the fleeing. fiyah: group, achar: kill, jarih: in the sense of passive participle, wounded, muwalli: derived from wali, one who turns his back fleeing. [3096] (117) Do not arrest their offspring and do not divide their property. The Muslim rebel's offspring should not be captured and made slaves, and their property should not be taken as ghanimah and distributed. These people are Muslims, so their offspring cannot be enslaved, and their property cannot be divided. (2) There is proof in an athar: Ali commanded his crier to announce on the day of Basra: Do not pursue the fleeing, do not finish off the wounded, do not kill the prisoner; whoever closes his door is safe, whoever throws down his weapon is safe; and do not take anything from their belongings. (b) And in another narration: Ali was asked about taking the offspring of rebels as captives, and he said: There is no taking captives for them; we only fought those who fought us. (c) (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Bab Ahl al-Baghi, vol. 8, p. 1314, no. 16747/16749) From these two athars it is learned that their offspring cannot be enslaved and their property cannot be divided. tasmi: derived from sabi, to take captive; dhurriyyah: offspring.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This section contains two distinct rulings: first, that the offspring of rebels cannot be taken as captives (enslaved) and their property cannot be divided as spoils of war (ghanimah); second, that it is permissible for Muslim troops to use the rebels' own weapons against them if needed. The commentary explains that because rebels are Muslims, their children are immune to enslavement and their property is protected. The proof is from the practice of Ali during the Battle of Basra (Jamal) where he announced not to pursue, not to kill wounded or prisoners, and not to take their belongings. Also, Ali was specifically asked about taking the offspring of rebels captive and he replied that there is no captivity for them; they only fought those who fought them. The hadith of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud is also cited as a general principle that rebels are not to be pursued, nor their prisoners killed, nor their wounded finished off, because they are Muslims. However, the commentary adds a qualification: if there is a group behind them, then it is permissible to arrest and pursue to prevent further fitna.

Key Terms
سبى (sabi): Taking captives, enslavement
ذرية (dhurriyyah): Offspring, children
قسم مال (qism mal): Dividing property as spoils (ghanimah) among the army
مدبر (mudbir): One who flees from battle, turns his back
جريح (jarih): Wounded person

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because they are not captured in battle
B) Because they are Muslims
C) Because they have no value
D) Because they are protected by the state

Key Takeaway
Rebels are Muslims: their offspring cannot be enslaved and their property cannot be divided, confirming the inviolability of Muslim life and wealth.
